class="firstHeading" lang="en">Ice age</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ice_Age&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a>)</span></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about a generic geological period of temperature reduction. For the most recent glacial period commonly referred to as the Ice Age, see <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">Last glacial period</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Ice_age_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Ice age (disambiguation)">Ice age (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IceAgeEarth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/IceAgeEarth.jpg/300px-IceAgeEarth.jpg" width="300" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/IceAgeEarth.jpg/450px-IceAgeEarth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/IceAgeEarth.jpg/600px-IceAgeEarth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8432" data-file-height="8432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IceAgeEarth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An artist\'s impression of ice age Earth at glacial maximum. Based on: <cite class="citation journal">Crowley, T. J. (1995). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.agu.org/pubs/crossref/1995/95GB01107.shtml">"Ice age terrestrial carbon changes revisited"</a>. <i>Global Biogeochemical Cycles</i>. <b>9</b> (3): 377\xe2\x80\x93389. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1995GBioC...9..377C">1995GBioC...9..377C</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1029%2F95GB01107">10.1029/95GB01107</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Global+Biogeochemical+Cycles&amp;rft.atitle=Ice+age+terrestrial+carbon+changes+revisited&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=377-389&amp;rft.date=1995&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1029%2F95GB01107&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1995GBioC...9..377C&amp;rft.au=Crowley%2C+T.+J.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.agu.org%2Fpubs%2Fcrossref%2F1995%2F95GB01107.shtml&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIce+age" class="Z3988"></span><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r861714446">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output q{quotes:"\\"""\\"""\'""\'"}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:inherit;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-free a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-registration a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-gray-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-red-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration{color:#555}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription span,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration span{border-bottom:1px dotted;cursor:help}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration,.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style></div></div></div>\n<p>An <b>ice age</b> is a long period of reduction in the temperature of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>\'s surface and atmosphere, resulting in the presence or expansion of continental and polar <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheets</a> and alpine <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>. Within a long-term ice age, individual pulses of cold climate are termed "<a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glacial periods</a>" (or, alternatively, "glacials" or "glaciations", or colloquially "ice ages"), and intermittent warm periods are called "<a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacials</a>". \n</p><p>In the terminology of <a href="/wiki/Glaciology" title="Glaciology">glaciology</a>, <i>ice age</i> implies the presence of extensive ice sheets in both northern and southern hemispheres.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> By this definition, we are in an interglacial period\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>\xe2\x80\x94of the current ice age. \n</p><p>The current ice age began 2.6 million years ago at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> epoch, and the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheets</a> still exist.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:22.519823788546em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.85em; left:-14em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="position: absolute; left: -000px; top: 000px"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:3.537px 10px 3.537px 10px;"><a href="/wiki/Mesoarchean" title="Mesoarchean"><span z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="position: absolute; left: 002px; top: 000px"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:14px 4px 14px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Huronian_glaciation" title="Huronian glaciation"><span margin-top:5.3722466960352em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-1.15em; left:-13.5em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; margin-top:2.4581497797357em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-1em; left:-13.8em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Karoo_Ice_Age" title="Karoo Ice Age"><span style="color:black;"><small>Karoo</small></span></a></span></span></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:0.010308370044053em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.28em; left:-13.55em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; margin-top:0em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-1.5em; left:-13.5em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"><span style="font:bold 9px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_glaciation" title="Timeline of glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><b>Ice_Ages</b></span></a></span></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div></div></div><div id="Legend" class="toccolours" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div><div id="Caption" class="toccolours" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><div style="float: right;"><span style="font-size:85%;">(<a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">view</a> \xe2\x80\xa2 <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Life_timeline" title="Template talk:Life timeline">discuss</a>)</span></div><div style="float: left;"><small><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#One_dimension" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">million years</a></small></div><br /><span style="float:right;margin-right:0.5em"><img alt="The image above contains clickable links" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/18px-Interactive_icon.svg.png" title="The image above contains clickable links" width="18" height="27" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/27px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/36px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="133" data-file-height="200" /></span><div style="float: left;"><small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1742 Pierre Martel (1706\xe2\x80\x931767), an engineer and geographer living in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, visited the valley of <a href="/wiki/Chamonix" title="Chamonix">Chamonix</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> of <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">Savoy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Two years later he published an account of his journey. He reported that the inhabitants of that valley attributed the dispersal of <a href="/wiki/Glacial_erratic" title="Glacial erratic">erratic boulders</a> to the glaciers, saying that they had once extended much farther.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Later similar explanations were reported from other regions of the Alps. In 1815 the carpenter and <a href="/wiki/Chamois" title="Chamois">chamois</a> hunter Jean-Pierre Perraudin (1767\xe2\x80\x931858) explained erratic boulders in the Val de Bagnes in the Swiss canton of Valais as being due to glaciers previously extending further.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> An unknown woodcutter from Meiringen in the Bernese Oberland advocated a similar idea in a discussion with the Swiss-German geologist <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Charpentier" title="Jean de Charpentier">Jean de Charpentier</a> (1786\xe2\x80\x931855) in 1834.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Comparable explanations are also known from the Val de Ferret in the Valais and the Seeland in western Switzerland<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Goethean_science" title="Goethean science">scientific work</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Such explanations could also be found in other parts of the world. When the Bavarian naturalist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_von_Bibra" title="Ernst von Bibra">Ernst von Bibra</a> (1806\xe2\x80\x931878) visited the Chilean Andes in 1849\xe2\x80\x931850, the natives attributed fossil <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">moraines</a> to the former action of glaciers.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, European scholars had begun to wonder what had caused the dispersal of erratic material. From the middle of the 18th century, some discussed ice as a means of transport. The Swedish mining expert Daniel Tilas (1712\xe2\x80\x931772) was, in 1742, the first person to suggest drifting sea ice in order to explain the presence of erratic boulders in the Scandinavian and Baltic regions.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1795, the Scottish philosopher and gentleman naturalist, <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a> (1726\xe2\x80\x931797), explained erratic boulders in the Alps by the action of glaciers.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Two decades later, in 1818, the Swedish botanist <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ran_Wahlenberg" title="G\xc3\xb6ran Wahlenberg">G\xc3\xb6ran Wahlenberg</a> (1780\xe2\x80\x931851) published his theory of a glaciation of the Scandinavian peninsula. He regarded glaciation as a regional phenomenon.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Only a few years later, the Danish-Norwegian geologist <a href="/wiki/Jens_Esmark" title="Jens Esmark">Jens Esmark</a> (1762\xe2\x80\x931839) argued a sequence of worldwide ice ages. In a paper published in 1824, Esmark proposed changes in climate as the cause of those glaciations. He attempted to show that they originated from changes in Earth\'s orbit.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> During the following years, Esmark\'s ideas were discussed and taken over in parts by Swedish, Scottish and German scientists. At the University of Edinburgh <a href="/wiki/Robert_Jameson" title="Robert Jameson">Robert Jameson</a> (1774\xe2\x80\x931854) seemed to be relatively open to Esmark\'s ideas, as reviewed by Norwegian professor of glaciology <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rn_G._Andersen" title="Bj\xc3\xb8rn G. Andersen">Bj\xc3\xb8rn G. Andersen</a> (1992).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Jameson\'s remarks about ancient glaciers in Scotland were most probably prompted by Esmark.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In Germany, Albrecht Reinhard Bernhardi (1797\xe2\x80\x931849), a geologist and professor of forestry at an academy in Dreissigacker, since incorporated in the southern <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Meiningen" title="Meiningen">Meiningen</a>, adopted Esmark\'s theory. In a paper published in 1832, Bernhardi speculated about former polar ice caps reaching as far as the temperate zones of the globe.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1829, independently of these debates, the Swiss civil engineer <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Venetz" title="Ignaz Venetz">Ignaz Venetz</a> (1788\xe2\x80\x931859) explained the dispersal of erratic boulders in the Alps, the nearby Jura Mountains, and the North German Plain as being due to huge glaciers. When he read his paper before the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Academy_of_Natural_Sciences_Switzerland&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Academy of Natural Sciences Switzerland (page does not exist)">Schweizerische Naturforschende Gesellschaft</a>, most scientists remained sceptical.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Finally, Venetz convinced his friend Jean de Charpentier. De Charpentier transformed Venetz\'s idea into a theory with a glaciation limited to the Alps. His thoughts resembled Wahlenberg\'s theory. In fact, both men shared the same volcanistic, or in de Charpentier\'s case rather <a href="/wiki/Plutonism" title="Plutonism">plutonistic</a> assumptions, about the Earth\'s history. In 1834, de Charpentier presented his paper before the Schweizerische Naturforschende Gesellschaft.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In the meantime, the German botanist <a href="/wiki/Karl_Friedrich_Schimper" title="Karl Friedrich Schimper">Karl Friedrich Schimper</a> (1803\xe2\x80\x931867) was studying mosses which were growing on erratic boulders in the alpine upland of Bavaria. He began to wonder where such masses of stone had come from. During the summer of 1835 he made some excursions to the Bavarian Alps. Schimper came to the conclusion that ice must have been the means of transport for the boulders in the alpine upland. In the winter of 1835 to 1836 he held some lectures in Munich. Schimper then assumed that there must have been global times of obliteration ("Ver\xc3\xb6dungszeiten") with a cold climate and frozen water.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Schimper spent the summer months of 1836 at Devens, near Bex, in the Swiss Alps with his former university friend <a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz" title="Louis Agassiz">Louis Agassiz</a> (1801\xe2\x80\x931873) and Jean de Charpentier. Schimper, de Charpentier and possibly Venetz convinced Agassiz that there had been a time of glaciation. During the winter of 1836/37, Agassiz and Schimper developed the theory of a sequence of glaciations. They mainly drew upon the preceding works of Venetz, de Charpentier and on their own fieldwork. Agassiz appears to have been already familiar with Bernhardi\'s paper at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> At the beginning of 1837, Schimper coined the term "ice age" (<i>"Eiszeit"</i>) for the period of the glaciers.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In July 1837 Agassiz presented their synthesis before the annual meeting of the Schweizerische Naturforschende Gesellschaft at Neuch\xc3\xa2tel. The audience was very critical and some opposed to the new theory because it contradicted the established opinions on climatic history. Most contemporary scientists thought that the Earth had been gradually cooling down since its birth as a molten globe.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In order to overcome this rejection, Agassiz embarked on geological fieldwork. He published his book <i>Study on Glaciers</i> ("\xc3\x89tudes sur les glaciers") in 1840.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> De Charpentier was put out by this, as he had also been preparing a book about the glaciation of the Alps. De Charpentier felt that Agassiz should have given him precedence as it was he who had introduced Agassiz to in-depth glacial research.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Besides that, Agassiz had, as a result of personal quarrels, omitted any mention of Schimper in his book.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>All together, it took several decades until the ice age theory was fully accepted by scientists. This happened on an international scale in the second half of the 1870s following the work of <a href="/wiki/James_Croll" title="James Croll">James Croll</a>, including the publication of <i>Climate and Time, in Their Geological Relations</i> in 1875, which provided a credible explanation for the causes of ice ages.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence_for_ice_ages">Evidence for ice ages</span></h2>\n<p>There are three main types of evidence for ice ages: geological, chemical, and paleontological.\n</p><p><b>Geological</b> evidence for ice ages comes in various forms, including rock scouring and scratching, <a href="/wiki/Glacial_moraine" class="mw-redirect" title="Glacial moraine">glacial moraines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drumlin" title="Drumlin">drumlins</a>, valley cutting, and the deposition of <a href="/wiki/Till" title="Till">till</a> or tillites and <a href="/wiki/Glacial_erratic" title="Glacial erratic">glacial erratics</a>. Successive glaciations tend to distort and erase the geological evidence, making it difficult to interpret. Furthermore, this evidence was difficult to date exactly; early theories assumed that the glacials were short compared to the long interglacials. The advent of sediment and ice cores revealed the true situation: glacials are long, interglacials short. It took some time for the current theory to be worked out.\n</p><p>The <b>chemical</b> evidence mainly consists of variations in the ratios of <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> in fossils present in sediments and sedimentary rocks and ocean sediment cores. For the most recent glacial periods <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice cores</a> provide climate <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">proxies</a> from their ice, and atmospheric samples from included bubbles of air. Because water containing heavier isotopes has a higher <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_evaporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat of evaporation">heat of evaporation</a>, its proportion decreases with colder conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> This allows a temperature record to be constructed. This evidence can be confounded, however, by other factors recorded by isotope ratios.\n</p><p>The <b>paleontological</b> evidence consists of changes in the geographical distribution of fossils. During a glacial period cold-adapted organisms spread into lower latitudes, and organisms that prefer warmer conditions become extinct or are squeezed into lower latitudes. This evidence is also difficult to interpret because it requires (1) sequences of sediments covering a long period of time, over a wide range of latitudes and which are easily correlated; (2) ancient organisms which survive for several million years without change and whose temperature preferences are easily diagnosed; and (3) the finding of the relevant fossils.\n</p><p>Despite the difficulties, analysis of ice core and ocean sediment cores<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> has shown periods of glacials and interglacials over the past few million years. These also confirm the linkage between ice ages and continental crust phenomena such as glacial moraines, drumlins, and glacial erratics. Hence the continental crust phenomena are accepted as good evidence of earlier ice ages when they are found in layers created much earlier than the time range for which ice cores and ocean sediment cores are available.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_ice_ages">Major ice ages</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:612px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GlaciationsinEarthExistancelicenced_annotated.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/GlaciationsinEarthExistancelicenced_annotated.jpg/610px-GlaciationsinEarthExistancelicenced_annotated.jpg" width="610" height="47" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/GlaciationsinEarthExistancelicenced_annotated.jpg/915px-GlaciationsinEarthExistancelicenced_annotated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/GlaciationsinEarthExistancelicenced_annotated.jpg/1220px-GlaciationsinEarthExistancelicenced_annotated.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3316" data-file-height="256" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GlaciationsinEarthExistancelicenced_annotated.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Timeline of glaciations, shown in blue.</div></div></div>\n<p>There have been at least five major ice ages in the Earth\'s history (the <a href="/wiki/Huronian" class="mw-redirect" title="Huronian">Huronian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian">Cryogenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andean-Saharan" class="mw-redirect" title="Andean-Saharan">Andean-Saharan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karoo_Ice_Age" title="Karoo Ice Age">Karoo Ice Age</a>, and the current <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary Ice Age</a>). Outside these ages, the Earth seems to have been ice free even in high latitudes.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EisrandlagenNorddeutschland.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/EisrandlagenNorddeutschland.png/220px-EisrandlagenNorddeutschland.png" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/EisrandlagenNorddeutschland.png/330px-EisrandlagenNorddeutschland.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/EisrandlagenNorddeutschland.png/440px-EisrandlagenNorddeutschland.png 2x" data-file-width="474" data-file-height="405" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EisrandlagenNorddeutschland.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ice age map of northern Germany and its northern neighbours. Red: maximum limit of <a href="/wiki/Weichselian" class="mw-redirect" title="Weichselian">Weichselian</a> glacial; yellow: <a href="/wiki/Saale_glaciation" title="Saale glaciation">Saale</a> glacial at maximum (Drenthe stage); blue: <a href="/wiki/Anglian_glaciation" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglian glaciation">Elster</a> glacial maximum glaciation.</div></div></div><p>Rocks from the earliest well established ice age, called the <a href="/wiki/Huronian" class="mw-redirect" title="Huronian">Huronian</a>, formed around 2.4 to 2.1 <a href="/wiki/Gigaanna" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigaanna">Ga</a> (<a href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> years) ago during the early <a href="/wiki/Proterozoic" title="Proterozoic">Proterozoic</a> Eon. Several hundreds of km of the <a href="/wiki/Huronian_Supergroup" title="Huronian Supergroup">Huronian Supergroup</a> are exposed 10\xe2\x80\x93100&#160;km north of the north shore of Lake Huron extending from near Sault Ste. Marie to Sudbury, northeast of Lake Huron, with giant layers of now-lithified till beds, dropstones, varves, outwash, and scoured basement rocks. Correlative Huronian deposits have been found near <a href="/wiki/Marquette,_Michigan" title="Marquette, Michigan">Marquette, Michigan</a>, and correlation has been made with Paleoproterozoic glacial deposits from Western Australia. The Huronian ice age was caused by the elimination of atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" title="Great Oxygenation Event">Great Oxygenation Event</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The next well-documented ice age, and probably the most severe of the last billion years, occurred from 850 to 630 million years ago (the <a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian">Cryogenian</a> period) and may have produced a <a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">Snowball Earth</a> in which glacial ice sheets reached the equator,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> possibly being ended by the accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> such as CO<sub>2</sub> produced by volcanoes. "The presence of ice on the continents and pack ice on the oceans would inhibit both <a href="/wiki/Silicate_mineral" class="mw-redirect" title="Silicate mineral">silicate</a> <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, which are the two major sinks for CO<sub>2</sub> at present."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> It has been suggested that the end of this ice age was responsible for the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran" title="Ediacaran">Ediacaran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, though this model is recent and controversial.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Andean-Saharan" class="mw-redirect" title="Andean-Saharan">Andean-Saharan</a> occurred from 460 to 420 million years ago, during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Ordovician" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Ordovician">Late Ordovician</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a> period.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg/300px-Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg.png" width="300" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg/450px-Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg/600px-Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="372" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Five_Myr_Climate_Change.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sediment records showing the fluctuating sequences of glacials and interglacials during the last several million years.</div></div></div>\n<p>The evolution of land plants at the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period caused a long term increase in planetary oxygen levels and reduction of CO<sub>2</sub> levels, which resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Karoo_Ice_Age" title="Karoo Ice Age">Karoo Ice Age</a>. It is named after the glacial tills found in the Karoo region of South Africa, where evidence for this ice age was first clearly identified. There were extensive polar <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">ice caps</a> at intervals from 360 to 260 million years ago in South Africa during the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">early Permian</a> Periods. Correlatives are known from Argentina, also in the center of the ancient supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Gondwanaland" class="mw-redirect" title="Gondwanaland">Gondwanaland</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary Glaciation / Quaternary Ice Age</a> started about 2.58 million years ago at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary Period</a> when the spread of ice sheets in the Northern Hemisphere began. Since then, the world has seen cycles of glaciation with ice sheets advancing and retreating on 40,000- and 100,000-year time scales called <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glacial periods</a>, glacials or glacial advances, and <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacial</a> periods, interglacials or glacial retreats. The earth is currently in an interglacial, and the last glacial period ended about 10,000 years ago. All that remains of the continental <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheets</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheets</a> and smaller glaciers such as on <a href="/wiki/Baffin_Island" title="Baffin Island">Baffin Island</a>.\n</p><p>The definition of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a> as beginning 2.58 Ma is based on the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ice_cap" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic ice cap">Arctic ice cap</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a> began to form earlier, at about 34 Ma, in the mid-<a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Eocene%E2%80%93Oligocene_extinction_event" title="Eocene\xe2\x80\x93Oligocene extinction event">Eocene-Oligocene Boundary</a>). The term <a href="/wiki/Late_Cenozoic_Ice_Age" title="Late Cenozoic Ice Age">Late Cenozoic Ice Age</a> is used to include this early phase.<sup id="cite_ref-UHCL_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UHCL-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ice ages can be further divided by location and time; for example, the names <i>Riss</i> (180,000\xe2\x80\x93130,000 years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">bp</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrm_glaciation" title="W\xc3\xbcrm glaciation">W\xc3\xbcrm</a></i> (70,000\xe2\x80\x9310,000 years bp) refer specifically to glaciation in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alpine region</a>. The maximum extent of the ice is not maintained for the full interval. The scouring action of each glaciation tends to remove most of the evidence of prior ice sheets almost completely, except in regions where the later sheet does not achieve full coverage.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Glacials_and_interglacials">Glacials and interglacials</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">Glacial period</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">Interglacial</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ice_Age_Temperature.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ice_Age_Temperature.png/300px-Ice_Age_Temperature.png" width="300" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ice_Age_Temperature.png/450px-Ice_Age_Temperature.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f8/Ice_Age_Temperature.png 2x" data-file-width="564" data-file-height="377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ice_Age_Temperature.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shows the pattern of temperature and ice volume changes associated with recent glacials and interglacials</div></div></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Minimum and maximum glaciation</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceage_north-intergl_glac_hg.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Iceage_north-intergl_glac_hg.png/230px-Iceage_north-intergl_glac_hg.png" width="230" height="230" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Iceage_north-intergl_glac_hg.png/345px-Iceage_north-intergl_glac_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Iceage_north-intergl_glac_hg.png/460px-Iceage_north-intergl_glac_hg.png 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="3000" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Minimum (interglacial, black) and maximum (glacial, grey) glaciation of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">northern hemisphere</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceage_south-intergl_glac_hg.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Iceage_south-intergl_glac_hg.png/230px-Iceage_south-intergl_glac_hg.png" width="230" height="230" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Iceage_south-intergl_glac_hg.png/345px-Iceage_south-intergl_glac_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Iceage_south-intergl_glac_hg.png/460px-Iceage_south-intergl_glac_hg.png 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="3000" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Minimum (interglacial, black) and maximum (glacial, grey) glaciation of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">southern hemisphere</a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>Within the ice ages (or at least within the current one), more temperate and more severe periods occur. The colder periods are called <i>glacial periods</i>, the warmer periods <i>interglacials</i>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Eemian" title="Eemian">Eemian Stage</a>.\n</p><p>Glacials are characterized by cooler and drier climates over most of the earth and large land and sea ice masses extending outward from the poles. Mountain glaciers in otherwise unglaciated areas extend to lower elevations due to a lower <a href="/wiki/Snow_line" title="Snow line">snow line</a>. Sea levels drop due to the removal of large volumes of water above sea level in the icecaps. There is evidence that ocean circulation patterns are disrupted by glaciations. Since the earth has significant continental glaciation in the Arctic and Antarctic, we are currently in a glacial minimum of a glaciation. Such a period between glacial maxima is known as an <i>interglacial</i>. The glacials and interglacials also coincided with changes in Earth\'s orbit called <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">Milankovitch cycles</a>.\n</p><p>The earth has been in an interglacial period known as the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> for around 11,700 years,<sup id="cite_ref-Walker,_M._2009._pp._3_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker,_M._2009._pp._3-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and an article in <i>Nature</i> in 2004 argues that it might be most analogous to a previous interglacial that lasted 28,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Predicted changes in <a href="/wiki/Orbital_forcing" title="Orbital forcing">orbital forcing</a> suggest that the next glacial period would begin at least 50,000 years from now, even in absence of human-made <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a><sup id="cite_ref-BergerLoutre_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BergerLoutre-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">Milankovitch cycles</a>). Moreover, anthropogenic forcing from increased <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> might outweigh orbital forcing for as long as intensive use of fossil fuels continues.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive_and_negative_feedback_in_glacial_periods">Positive and negative feedback in glacial periods</span></h2>\n<p>Each glacial period is subject to <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> which makes it more severe, and <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">negative feedback</a> which mitigates and (in all cases so far) eventually ends it.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive_feedback_processes">Positive feedback processes</span></h3>\n<p>Ice and snow increase Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>, i.e. they make it reflect more of the sun\'s energy and absorb less. Hence, when the air temperature decreases, ice and snow fields grow, and this continues until competition with a negative feedback mechanism forces the system to an equilibrium. Also, the reduction in <a href="/wiki/Boreal_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Boreal forest">forests</a> caused by the ice\'s expansion increases albedo.\n</p><p>Another theory proposed by Ewing and Donn in 1956<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> hypothesized that an ice-free Arctic Ocean leads to increased snowfall at high latitudes. When low-temperature ice covers the Arctic Ocean there is little evaporation or <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a> and the polar regions are quite dry in terms of precipitation, comparable to the amount found in mid-latitude <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a>. This low precipitation allows high-latitude snowfalls to melt during the summer. An ice-free Arctic Ocean absorbs solar radiation during the long summer days, and evaporates more water into the Arctic atmosphere. With higher precipitation, portions of this snow may not melt during the summer and so glacial ice can form at lower altitudes <i>and</i> more southerly latitudes, reducing the temperatures over land by increased albedo as noted above. Furthermore, under this hypothesis the lack of oceanic pack ice allows increased exchange of waters between the Arctic and the North Atlantic Oceans, warming the Arctic and cooling the North Atlantic. (Current projected consequences of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> include a largely ice-free Arctic Ocean within 5\xe2\x80\x9320 years, see <a href="/wiki/Arctic_shrinkage" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic shrinkage">Arctic shrinkage</a>.) Additional fresh water flowing into the North Atlantic during a warming cycle may also <a href="/wiki/Shutdown_of_thermohaline_circulation" title="Shutdown of thermohaline circulation">reduce</a> the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">global ocean water circulation</a>. Such a reduction (by reducing the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>) would have a cooling effect on northern Europe, which in turn would lead to increased low-latitude snow retention during the summer. It has also been suggested that during an extensive glacial, glaciers may move through the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Saint_Lawrence" title="Gulf of Saint Lawrence">Gulf of Saint Lawrence</a>, extending into the North Atlantic Ocean far enough to block the Gulf Stream.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Negative_feedback_processes">Negative feedback processes</span></h3>\n<p>Ice sheets that form during glaciations cause erosion of the land beneath them. After some time, this will reduce land above sea level and thus diminish the amount of space on which ice sheets can form. This mitigates the albedo feedback, as does the lowering in sea level that accompanies the formation of ice sheets.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Another factor is the increased aridity occurring with glacial maxima, which reduces the precipitation available to maintain glaciation. The glacial retreat induced by this or any other process can be amplified by similar <a href="/w/index.php?title=Inverse_positive_feedback&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Inverse positive feedback (page does not exist)">inverse positive feedbacks</a> as for glacial advances.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to research published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Geoscience" title="Nature Geoscience">Nature Geoscience</a></i>, human emissions of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>)</a> will defer the next ice age. Researchers used data on Earth\'s orbit to find the historical warm interglacial period that looks most like the current one and from this have predicted that the next ice age would usually begin within 1,500 years. They go on to say that emissions have been so high that it will not.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_ice_ages">Causes of ice ages</span></h2>\n<p>The causes of ice ages are not fully understood for either the large-scale ice age periods or the smaller ebb and flow of glacial\xe2\x80\x93interglacial periods within an ice age. The consensus is that several factors are important: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmospheric composition</a>, such as the concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (the specific levels of the previously mentioned gases are now able to be seen with the new ice core samples from EPICA Dome C in Antarctica over the past 800,000 years); changes in the earth\'s orbit around the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">Milankovitch cycles</a>; the motion of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">tectonic plates</a> resulting in changes in the relative location and amount of continental and oceanic crust on the earth\'s surface, which affect wind and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">ocean currents</a>; variations in <a href="/wiki/Solar_variation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar variation">solar output</a>; the orbital dynamics of the Earth\xe2\x80\x93Moon system; the impact of relatively large <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> and volcanism including eruptions of <a href="/wiki/Supervolcano" title="Supervolcano">supervolcanoes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Some of these factors influence each other. For example, changes in Earth\'s atmospheric composition (especially the concentrations of greenhouse gases) may alter the climate, while climate change itself can change the atmospheric composition (for example by changing the rate at which <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> removes CO<sub>2</sub>).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Maureen_Raymo" title="Maureen Raymo">Maureen Raymo</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Ruddiman" title="William Ruddiman">William Ruddiman</a> and others propose that the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Plateau" title="Colorado Plateau">Colorado Plateaus</a> are immense CO<sub>2</sub> "scrubbers" with a capacity to remove enough CO<sub>2</sub> from the global atmosphere to be a significant causal factor of the 40 million year <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic#Climate" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic Cooling</a> trend. They further claim that approximately half of their uplift (and CO<sub>2</sub> "scrubbing" capacity) occurred in the past 10 million years.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Raymo,_Ruddiman_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raymo,_Ruddiman-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Changes_in_Earth.27s_atmosphere"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_Earth\'s_atmosphere">Changes in Earth\'s atmosphere</span></h3>\n<p>There is evidence that <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> levels fell at the start of ice ages and rose during the retreat of the ice sheets, but it is difficult to establish cause and effect (see the notes above on the role of weathering). Greenhouse gas levels may also have been affected by other factors which have been proposed as causes of ice ages, such as the movement of continents and volcanism.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">Snowball Earth</a> hypothesis maintains that the severe freezing in the late <a href="/wiki/Proterozoic" title="Proterozoic">Proterozoic</a> was ended by an increase in CO<sub>2</sub> levels in the atmosphere, mainly from volcanoes, and some supporters of Snowball Earth argue that it was caused in the first place by a reduction in atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub>. The hypothesis also warns of future Snowball Earths.\n</p><p>In 2009, further evidence was provided that changes in solar <a href="/wiki/Insolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Insolation">insolation</a> provide the initial trigger for the earth to warm after an Ice Age, with secondary factors like increases in greenhouse gases accounting for the magnitude of the change.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Human-induced_changes">Human-induced changes</span></h4>\n<p>There is considerable evidence that over the very recent period of the last 100\xe2\x80\x931000 years, the sharp increases in human activity, especially the burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, has caused the parallel sharp and accelerating increase in atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> which trap the sun\'s heat. The consensus theory of the scientific community is that the resulting <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a> is a principal cause of the increase in <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> which has occurred over the same period, and a chief contributor to the accelerated melting of the remaining <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polar_ice" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar ice">polar ice</a>. A 2012 investigation finds that dinosaurs released methane through digestion in a similar amount to humanity\'s current methane release, which "could have been a key factor" to the very warm climate 150 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>William Ruddiman has proposed the <a href="/wiki/Early_anthropocene" title="Early anthropocene">early anthropocene</a> hypothesis, according to which the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">anthropocene</a> era, as some people call the most recent period in the earth\'s history when the activities of the human species first began to have a significant global impact on the earth\'s climate and ecosystems, did not begin in the 18th century with the advent of the Industrial Era, but dates back to 8,000 years ago, due to intense farming activities of our early agrarian ancestors. It was at that time that atmospheric greenhouse gas concentrations stopped following the periodic pattern of the <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">Milankovitch cycles</a>. In his <a href="/wiki/Overdue-glaciation" class="mw-redirect" title="Overdue-glaciation">overdue-glaciation</a> hypothesis Ruddiman states that an incipient glacial would probably have begun several thousand years ago, but the arrival of that scheduled glacial was forestalled by the activities of early farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At a meeting of the <a href="/wiki/American_Geophysical_Union" title="American Geophysical Union">American Geophysical Union</a> (December 17, 2008), scientists detailed evidence in support of the controversial idea that the introduction of large-scale rice agriculture in Asia, coupled with extensive deforestation in Europe began to alter world climate by pumping significant amounts of greenhouse gases into the atmosphere over the last 1,000 years. In turn, a warmer atmosphere heated the oceans making them much less efficient storehouses of carbon dioxide and reinforcing global warming, possibly forestalling the onset of a new glacial age.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Position_of_the_continents">Position of the continents</span></h3>\n<p>The geological record appears to show that ice ages start when the continents are in <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">positions</a> which block or reduce the flow of warm water from the equator to the poles and thus allow ice sheets to form. The ice sheets increase Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">reflectivity</a> and thus reduce the absorption of solar radiation. With less radiation absorbed the atmosphere cools; the cooling allows the ice sheets to grow, which further increases reflectivity in a <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> loop. The ice age continues until the reduction in weathering causes an increase in the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>.\n</p><p>There are three main contributors from the layout of the continents that obstruct the movement of warm water to the poles:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>A continent sits on top of a pole, as <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> does today.</li>\n<li>A polar sea is almost land-locked, as the Arctic Ocean is today.</li>\n<li>A supercontinent covers most of the equator, as <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a> did during the <a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian">Cryogenian</a> period.</li></ul>\n<p>Since today\'s Earth has a continent over the South Pole and an almost land-locked ocean over the North Pole, geologists believe that Earth will continue to experience glacial periods in the geologically near future.\n</p><p>Some scientists believe that the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> are a major factor in the current ice age, because these mountains have increased Earth\'s total rainfall and therefore the rate at which carbon dioxide is washed out of the atmosphere, decreasing the greenhouse effect.<sup id="cite_ref-Raymo,_Ruddiman_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raymo,_Ruddiman-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The Himalayas\' formation started about 70 million years ago when the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian Plate</a> collided with the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a>, and the Himalayas are still rising by about 5&#160;mm per year because the Indo-Australian plate is still moving at 67&#160;mm/year. The history of the Himalayas broadly fits the long-term decrease in Earth\'s average temperature since the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">mid-Eocene</a>, 40 million years ago.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluctuations_in_ocean_currents">Fluctuations in ocean currents</span></h3>\n<p>Another important contribution to ancient climate regimes is the variation of <b>ocean currents</b>, which are modified by continent position, sea levels and salinity, as well as other factors. They have the ability to cool (e.g. aiding the creation of Antarctic ice) and the ability to warm (e.g. giving the British Isles a temperate as opposed to a boreal climate). The closing of the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a> about 3 million years ago may have ushered in the present period of strong glaciation over North America by ending the exchange of water between the tropical Atlantic and Pacific Oceans.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Analyses suggest that ocean current fluctuations can adequately account for recent glacial oscillations. During the last glacial period the sea-level has fluctuated 20\xe2\x80\x9330&#160;m as water was sequestered, primarily in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> ice sheets. When ice collected and the sea level dropped sufficiently, flow through the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a> (the narrow strait between Siberia and Alaska is about 50&#160;m deep today) was reduced, resulting in increased flow from the North Atlantic. This realigned the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> in the Atlantic, increasing heat transport into the Arctic, which melted the polar ice accumulation and reduced other continental ice sheets. The release of water raised sea levels again, restoring the ingress of colder water from the Pacific with an accompanying shift to northern hemisphere ice accumulation.<sup id="cite_ref-Hu2010_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hu2010-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uplift_of_the_Tibetan_plateau_and_surrounding_mountain_areas_above_the_snowline">Uplift of the Tibetan plateau and surrounding mountain areas above the snowline</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Matthias_Kuhle" title="Matthias Kuhle">Matthias Kuhle</a>\'s geological theory of Ice Age development was suggested by the existence of an ice sheet covering the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a> during the Ice Ages (<a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>?). According to Kuhle, the plate-tectonic uplift of Tibet past the snow-line has led to a surface of c. 2,400,000 square kilometres (930,000 sq mi) changing from bare land to ice with a 70% greater <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>. The reflection of energy into space resulted in a global cooling, triggering the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> Ice Age. Because this highland is at a subtropical latitude, with 4 to 5 times the insolation of high-latitude areas, what would be Earth\'s strongest heating surface has turned into a cooling surface.\n</p><p>Kuhle explains the <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacial</a> periods by the 100,000-year cycle of radiation changes due to variations in Earth\'s orbit. This comparatively insignificant warming, when combined with the lowering of the Nordic inland ice areas and Tibet due to the weight of the superimposed ice-load, has led to the repeated complete thawing of the inland ice areas.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Variations_in_Earth.27s_orbit_.28Milankovitch_cycles.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Variations_in_Earth\'s_orbit_(Milankovitch_cycles)">Variations in Earth\'s orbit (Milankovitch cycles)</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">Milankovitch cycles</a> are a set of cyclic variations in characteristics of the Earth\'s orbit around the Sun. Each cycle has a different length, so at some times their effects reinforce each other and at other times they (partially) cancel each other.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SummerSolstice65N-future.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/SummerSolstice65N-future.png/500px-SummerSolstice65N-future.png" width="500" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/SummerSolstice65N-future.png/750px-SummerSolstice65N-future.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/SummerSolstice65N-future.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="248" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SummerSolstice65N-future.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Past and future of daily average insolation at top of the atmosphere on the day of the summer solstice, at 65 N latitude.</div></div></div>\n<p>There is strong evidence that the Milankovitch cycles affect the occurrence of glacial and interglacial periods within an ice age. The present ice age is the most studied and best understood, particularly the last 400,000 years, since this is the period covered by <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice cores</a> that record atmospheric composition and proxies for temperature and ice volume. Within this period, the match of glacial/interglacial frequencies to the Milankovi\xc4\x87 orbital forcing periods is so close that orbital forcing is generally accepted. The combined effects of the changing distance to the Sun, the precession of the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Axis_of_rotation" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis of rotation">axis</a>, and the changing tilt of the Earth\'s axis redistribute the sunlight received by the Earth. Of particular importance are changes in the tilt of the Earth\'s axis, which affect the intensity of seasons. For example, the amount of solar influx in July at <a href="/wiki/65th_parallel_north" title="65th parallel north">65 degrees north</a> <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> varies by as much as 22% (from 450 W/m\xc2\xb2 to 550 W/m\xc2\xb2). It is widely believed that ice sheets advance when summers become too cool to melt all of the accumulated snowfall from the previous winter. Some believe that the strength of the orbital forcing is too small to trigger glaciations, but feedback mechanisms like CO<sub>2</sub> may explain this mismatch.\n</p><p>While Milankovitch forcing predicts that cyclic changes in the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbital elements</a> can be expressed in the glaciation record, additional explanations are necessary to explain which cycles are observed to be most important in the timing of glacial\xe2\x80\x93interglacial periods. In particular, during the last 800,000 years, the dominant period of glacial\xe2\x80\x93interglacial oscillation has been 100,000 years, which corresponds to <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">changes</a> in Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a> and orbital <a href="/wiki/Inclination" class="mw-redirect" title="Inclination">inclination</a>. Yet this is by far the weakest of the three frequencies predicted by Milankovitch. During the period 3.0\xe2\x80\x930.8 million years ago, the dominant pattern of glaciation corresponded to the 41,000-year period of changes in Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Obliquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Obliquity">obliquity</a> (tilt of the axis). The reasons for dominance of one frequency versus another are poorly understood and an active area of current research, but the answer probably relates to some form of resonance in the Earth\'s climate system.  Recent work suggests that the 100K year cycle dominates due to increased southern-pole sea-ice increasing total solar reflectivity.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The "traditional" Milankovitch explanation struggles to explain the dominance of the 100,000-year cycle over the last 8 cycles. <a href="/wiki/Richard_A._Muller" title="Richard A. Muller">Richard A. Muller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_J._F._MacDonald" title="Gordon J. F. MacDonald">Gordon J. F. MacDonald</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and others have pointed out that those calculations are for a two-dimensional orbit of Earth but the three-dimensional orbit also has a 100,000-year cycle of orbital inclination. They proposed that these variations in orbital inclination lead to variations in insolation, as the Earth moves in and out of known dust bands in the solar system. Although this is a different mechanism to the traditional view, the "predicted" periods over the last 400,000 years are nearly the same. The Muller and MacDonald theory, in turn, has been challenged by Jose Antonio Rial.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another worker, <a href="/wiki/William_Ruddiman" title="William Ruddiman">William Ruddiman</a>, has suggested a model that explains the 100,000-year cycle by the <a href="/wiki/Modulating" class="mw-redirect" title="Modulating">modulating</a> effect of eccentricity (weak 100,000-year cycle) on precession (26,000-year cycle) combined with greenhouse gas feedbacks in the 41,000- and 26,000-year cycles. Yet another theory has been advanced by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Huybers" title="Peter Huybers">Peter Huybers</a> who argued that the 41,000-year cycle has always been dominant, but that the Earth has entered a mode of climate behavior where only the second or third cycle triggers an ice age. This would imply that the 100,000-year periodicity is really an illusion created by averaging together cycles lasting 80,000 and 120,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> This theory is consistent with a simple empirical multi-state model proposed by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Didier_Paillard&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Didier Paillard (page does not exist)">Didier Paillard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Paillard suggests that the late Pleistocene glacial cycles\ncan be seen as jumps between three quasi-stable climate states. The jumps are induced by the <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbital</a> forcing, while in the early Pleistocene the 41,000-year glacial cycles resulted from jumps between only two climate states. A dynamical\nmodel explaining this behavior was proposed by Peter Ditlevsen.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> This is in support of the suggestion that the late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> glacial cycles are not due to the weak 100,000-year eccentricity cycle, but a non-linear response to mainly the 41,000-year obliquity cycle.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Variations_in_the_Sun.27s_energy_output"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Variations_in_the_Sun\'s_energy_output">Variations in the Sun\'s energy output</span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ice_age&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small><i>(January 2012)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>There are at least two types of variation in the Sun\'s energy output\n</p>\n<ul><li>In the very long term, astrophysicists believe that the Sun\'s output increases by about 7% every one billion (10<sup>9</sup>) years.</li>\n<li>Shorter-term variations such as <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspot cycles</a>, and longer episodes such as the <a href="/wiki/Maunder_Minimum" title="Maunder Minimum">Maunder Minimum</a>, which occurred during the coldest part of the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>.</li></ul>\n<p>The long-term increase in the Sun\'s output cannot be a cause of ice ages.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanism">Volcanism</span></h3>\n<p>Volcanic eruptions may have contributed to the inception and/or the end of ice age periods. At times during the paleoclimate, carbon dioxide levels were two or three times greater than today. Volcanoes and movements in continental plates contributed to high amounts of CO<sub>2</sub> in the atmosphere. Carbon dioxide from volcanoes probably contributed to periods with highest overall temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> One suggested explanation of the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene-Eocene_Thermal_Maximum" class="mw-redirect" title="Paleocene-Eocene Thermal Maximum">Paleocene-Eocene Thermal Maximum</a> is that undersea volcanoes released <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> from <a href="/wiki/Clathrate" class="mw-redirect" title="Clathrate">clathrates</a> and thus caused a large and rapid increase in the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>  There appears to be no geological evidence for such eruptions at the right time, but this does not prove they did not happen.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_glacial_and_interglacial_phases">Recent glacial and interglacial phases</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_glaciation" title="Timeline of glaciation">Timeline of glaciation</a></div>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>&#32;with: Recent glacial and interglacial phases in other areas outside North America. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ice_age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small><i>(March 2008)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_icesheet_hg.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Northern_icesheet_hg.png/280px-Northern_icesheet_hg.png" width="280" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Northern_icesheet_hg.png/420px-Northern_icesheet_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Northern_icesheet_hg.png/560px-Northern_icesheet_hg.png 2x" data-file-width="2236" data-file-height="1640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_icesheet_hg.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Northern hemisphere glaciation during the last ice ages.
