The setup of 3 to 4 kilometer thick ice sheets caused a <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level lowering</a> of about 120&#160;m.</div></div></div>\n<p>The current ice age, called the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary glaciation</a>, which began 2.6 million years ago and extends into the present,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> has seen more or less extensive glaciation on 40,000 and later, 100,000 year cycles. The <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacials</a> lasted about 10,000\xe2\x80\x9320,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> ended about 11,500 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Since then, Earth has been in an interglacial period called the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glacial_stages_in_North_America">Glacial stages in North America</span></h3>\n<p>The major glacial stages of the current ice age in North America are the <a href="/wiki/Illinoian_(stage)" title="Illinoian (stage)">Illinoian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eemian" title="Eemian">Eemian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_glaciation" title="Wisconsin glaciation">Wisconsin glaciation</a>. The use of the Nebraskan, Afton, Kansan, and Yarmouthian stages to subdivide the ice age in North America has been discontinued by Quaternary geologists and geomorphologists. These stages have all been merged into the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Illinoian" title="Pre-Illinoian">Pre-Illinoian</a> in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Hallberg1_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hallberg1-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RichmondOther1_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RichmondOther1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GibbardOthers2007_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GibbardOthers2007-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the most recent North American glaciation, during the latter part of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> (26,000 to 13,300 years ago), ice sheets extended to about <a href="/wiki/45th_parallel_north" title="45th parallel north">45th parallel north</a>. These sheets were 3 to 4 kilometres (1.9 to 2.5&#160;mi) thick.<sup id="cite_ref-RichmondOther1_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RichmondOther1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glacial_lakes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Glacial_lakes.jpg/220px-Glacial_lakes.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Glacial_lakes.jpg/330px-Glacial_lakes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Glacial_lakes.jpg/440px-Glacial_lakes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="545" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glacial_lakes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stages of <a href="/wiki/Proglacial_lake" title="Proglacial lake">proglacial lake</a> development in the region of the current North American <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>.</div></div></div> \n<p>This Wisconsin glaciation left widespread impacts on the North American landscape. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Finger_Lakes" title="Finger Lakes">Finger Lakes</a> were carved by ice deepening old valleys. Most of the lakes in Minnesota and Wisconsin were gouged out by glaciers and later filled with glacial meltwaters. The old <a href="/wiki/Teays_River" title="Teays River">Teays River</a> drainage system was radically altered and largely reshaped into the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> drainage system. Other rivers were dammed and diverted to new channels, such as <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls" title="Niagara Falls">Niagara Falls</a>, which formed a dramatic waterfall and gorge, when the waterflow encountered a limestone escarpment. Another similar waterfall, at the present <a href="/wiki/Clark_Reservation_State_Park" title="Clark Reservation State Park">Clark Reservation State Park</a> near <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_New_York" title="Syracuse, New York">Syracuse, New York</a>, is now dry.\n</p><p>The area from <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nantucket,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="Nantucket, Massachusetts">Nantucket, Massachusetts</a> was formed from glacial <a href="/wiki/Till" title="Till">till</a>, and the plethora of lakes on the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Shield" title="Canadian Shield">Canadian Shield</a> in northern Canada can be almost entirely attributed to the action of the ice. As the ice retreated and the rock dust dried, winds carried the material hundreds of miles, forming beds of <a href="/wiki/Loess" title="Loess">loess</a> many dozens of feet thick in the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_River" title="Missouri River">Missouri Valley</a>. <a href="/wiki/Post-glacial_rebound" title="Post-glacial rebound">Post-glacial rebound</a> continues to reshape the Great Lakes and other areas formerly under the weight of the ice sheets.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Driftless_Area" title="Driftless Area">Driftless Area</a>, a portion of western and southwestern Wisconsin along with parts of adjacent <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, was not covered by glaciers.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Glacial_history_of_Minnesota" title="Glacial history of Minnesota">Glacial history of Minnesota</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_Glacial_Period_in_the_semiarid_Andes_around_Aconcagua_and_Tupungato">Last Glacial Period in the semiarid Andes around Aconcagua and Tupungato</span></h3>\n<p>A specially interesting climatic change during glacial times has taken place in the semi-arid Andes. Beside the expected cooling down in comparison with the current climate, a significant precipitation change happened here. So, researches in the presently semiarid subtropic Aconcagua-massif (6,962&#160;m) have shown an unexpectedly extensive glacial glaciation of the type "ice stream network".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kuhle04_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle04-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kuhle11_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle11-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The connected valley glaciers exceeding 100&#160;km in length, flowed down on the East-side of this section of the Andes at 32\xe2\x80\x9334\xc2\xb0S and 69\xe2\x80\x9371\xc2\xb0W as far as a height of 2,060&#160;m and on the western luff-side still clearly deeper.<sup id="cite_ref-Kuhle11_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle11-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Where current glaciers scarcely reach 10&#160;km in length, the snowline (ELA) runs at a height of 4,600&#160;m and at that time was lowered to 3,200&#160;m <a href="/wiki/Above_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above sea level">asl</a>, i.e. about 1,400&#160;m. From this follows that\xe2\x80\x94beside of an annual depression of temperature about c. 8.4&#160;\xc2\xb0C\xe2\x80\x94 here was an increase in precipitation. Accordingly, at glacial times the humid climatic belt that today is situated several latitude degrees further to the S, was shifted much further to the N.<sup id="cite_ref-Kuhle04_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle04-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kuhle11_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle11-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_glaciation">Effects of glaciation</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scandinavia.TMO2003050.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Scandinavia.TMO2003050.jpg/220px-Scandinavia.TMO2003050.jpg" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Scandinavia.TMO2003050.jpg/330px-Scandinavia.TMO2003050.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Scandinavia.TMO2003050.jpg/440px-Scandinavia.TMO2003050.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5600" data-file-height="7200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scandinavia.TMO2003050.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> exhibits some of the typical effects of ice age glaciation such as <a href="/wiki/Fjord" title="Fjord">fjords</a> and lakes.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Glacial_landform" title="Glacial landform">Glacial landform</a></div>\n<p>Although the last glacial period ended more than 8,000 years ago, its effects can still be felt today. For example, the moving ice carved out the landscape in Canada (See <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Arctic_Archipelago" title="Canadian Arctic Archipelago">Canadian Arctic Archipelago</a>), Greenland, northern Eurasia and Antarctica. The <a href="/wiki/Erratic_boulder" class="mw-redirect" title="Erratic boulder">erratic boulders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Till" title="Till">till</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drumlin" title="Drumlin">drumlins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esker" title="Esker">eskers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fjord" title="Fjord">fjords</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kettle_lake" class="mw-redirect" title="Kettle lake">kettle lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">moraines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cirque" title="Cirque">cirques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glacial_horn" class="mw-redirect" title="Glacial horn">horns</a>, etc., are typical features left behind by the glaciers.\n</p><p>The weight of the ice sheets was so great that they deformed the Earth\'s crust and mantle. After the ice sheets melted, the ice-covered land <a href="/wiki/Post-glacial_rebound" title="Post-glacial rebound">rebounded</a>. Due to the high <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_mantle" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s mantle">Earth\'s mantle</a>, the flow of mantle rocks which controls the rebound process is very slow\xe2\x80\x94at a rate of about 1&#160;cm/year near the center of rebound area today.\n</p><p>During glaciation, water was taken from the oceans to form the ice at high latitudes, thus global sea level dropped by about 110 meters, exposing the continental shelves and forming land-bridges between land-masses for animals to migrate. During <a href="/wiki/Deglaciation" title="Deglaciation">deglaciation</a>, the melted ice-water returned to the oceans, causing sea level to rise. This process can cause sudden shifts in coastlines and hydration systems resulting in newly submerged lands, emerging lands, collapsed <a href="/wiki/Proglacial_lake" title="Proglacial lake">ice dams</a> resulting in <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salination</a> of lakes, new ice dams creating vast areas of freshwater, and a general alteration in regional weather patterns on a large but temporary scale. It can even cause temporary <a href="/wiki/Reglaciation" class="mw-redirect" title="Reglaciation">reglaciation</a>. This type of chaotic pattern of rapidly changing land, ice, saltwater and freshwater has been proposed as the likely model for the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> regions, as well as much of central North America at the end of the last glacial maximum, with the present-day coastlines only being achieved in the last few millennia of prehistory. Also, the effect of elevation on Scandinavia submerged a vast continental plain that had existed under much of what is now the North Sea, connecting the British Isles to Continental Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The redistribution of ice-water on the surface of the Earth and the flow of mantle rocks causes changes in the <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">gravitational field</a> as well as changes to the distribution of the <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia" title="Moment of inertia">moment of inertia</a> of the Earth. These changes to the moment of inertia result in a change in the <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">angular velocity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Axis_of_rotation" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis of rotation">axis</a>, and wobble of the Earth\'s rotation.\n</p><p>The weight of the redistributed surface mass loaded the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>, caused it to <a href="/wiki/Bending" title="Bending">flex</a> and also induced <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stress</a> within the Earth. The presence of the glaciers generally suppressed the movement of <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faults</a> below.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Deglaciation" title="Deglaciation">deglaciation</a>, the faults experience accelerated slip triggering <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a>. Earthquakes triggered near the ice margin may in turn accelerate <a href="/wiki/Ice_calving" title="Ice calving">ice calving</a> and may account for the <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_events" class="mw-redirect" title="Heinrich events">Heinrich events</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> As more ice is removed near the ice margin, more <a href="/wiki/Intraplate_earthquake" title="Intraplate earthquake">intraplate earthquakes</a> are induced and this positive feedback may explain the fast collapse of ice sheets.\n</p><p>In Europe, glacial erosion and <a href="/wiki/Isostasy" title="Isostasy">isostatic</a> sinking from weight of ice made the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, which before the Ice Age was all land drained by the <a href="/wiki/Eridanos_(geology)" title="Eridanos (geology)">Eridanos River</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Global_cooling" title="Global cooling">Global cooling</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Quaternary_Research" title="International Union for Quaternary Research">International Union for Quaternary Research</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea_Glacier" title="Irish Sea Glacier">Irish Sea Glacier</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Late_Glacial_Maximum" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Glacial Maximum">Late Glacial Maximum</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Post-glacial_rebound" title="Post-glacial rebound">Post-glacial rebound</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_glaciation" title="Timeline of glaciation">Timeline of glaciation</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Imbrie, J.;
